<p><b>Linus Carl Pauling</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;p&#39; in &#39;pie&#39;">p</span><span title="/ɔː/: &#39;au&#39; in &#39;fraud&#39;">ɔː</span><span title="&#39;l&#39; in &#39;lie&#39;">l</span><span title="/ɪ/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;kit&#39;">ɪ</span><span title="/ŋ/: &#39;ng&#39; in &#39;sing&#39;">ŋ</span></span>/</a></span></span>; February 28, 1901 – August 19, 1994)<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> was an American <a href="/wiki/Chemist" title="Chemist">chemist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biochemist" title="Biochemist">biochemist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chemical_engineer" title="Chemical engineer">chemical engineer</a>,  <a href="/wiki/Peace_activist" class="mw-redirect" title="Peace activist">peace activist</a>, author, and educator. He published more than 1,200 papers and books, of which about 850 dealt with scientific topics.<sup id="cite_ref-VolumeI_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VolumeI-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/New_Scientist" title="New Scientist">New Scientist</a></i> called him one of the 20 greatest scientists of all time,<sup id="cite_ref-Horgan_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horgan-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> and as of 2000, he was rated the 16th most important scientist in history.<sup id="cite_ref-Sci100_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sci100-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> <span class="anchor" id="Nobels"></span>For his scientific work, Pauling was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Chemistry" title="Nobel Prize in Chemistry">Nobel Prize in Chemistry</a> in 1954. For his peace activism, he was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Peace_Prize" title="Nobel Peace Prize">Nobel Peace Prize</a> in 1962. He is one of four individuals to have won more than one Nobel Prize (the others being <a href="/wiki/Marie_Curie" title="Marie Curie">Marie Curie</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Bardeen" title="John Bardeen">John Bardeen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Sanger" title="Frederick Sanger">Frederick Sanger</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Nobel_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nobel-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Of these, he is the only person to have been awarded two unshared Nobel Prizes,<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> and one of two people to be awarded Nobel Prizes in different fields, the other being <a href="/wiki/Marie_Curie" title="Marie Curie">Marie Curie</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Nobel_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nobel-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> He was married to the American human rights activist <a href="/wiki/Ava_Helen_Pauling" title="Ava Helen Pauling">Ava Helen Pauling</a>.
</p><p>Pauling was one of the founders of the fields of <a href="/wiki/Quantum_chemistry" title="Quantum chemistry">quantum chemistry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Molecular_biology" title="Molecular biology">molecular biology</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-natureobit_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-natureobit-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> His contributions to the theory of the chemical bond include the concept of <a href="/wiki/Orbital_hybridisation" title="Orbital hybridisation">orbital hybridisation</a> and the first accurate scale of <a href="/wiki/Electronegativity" title="Electronegativity">electronegativities</a> of the elements. Pauling also worked on the structures of biological molecules, and showed the importance of the <a href="/wiki/Alpha_helix" title="Alpha helix">alpha helix</a> and <a href="/wiki/Beta_sheet" title="Beta sheet">beta sheet</a> in <a href="/wiki/Protein_secondary_structure" title="Protein secondary structure">protein secondary structure</a>. Pauling's approach combined methods and results from <a href="/wiki/X-ray_crystallography" title="X-ray crystallography">X-ray crystallography</a>, <a href="/wiki/Molecular_model" title="Molecular model">molecular model</a> building, and <a href="/wiki/Quantum_chemistry" title="Quantum chemistry">quantum chemistry</a>. His discoveries inspired the work of <a href="/wiki/James_Watson" title="James Watson">James Watson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Francis_Crick" title="Francis Crick">Francis Crick</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Wilkins" title="Maurice Wilkins">Maurice Wilkins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rosalind_Franklin" title="Rosalind Franklin">Rosalind Franklin</a> on the structure of DNA, which in turn made it possible for geneticists to crack the DNA code of all organisms.<sup id="cite_ref-Contributions_to_DNA_double-helix_discovery_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Contributions_to_DNA_double-helix_discovery-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In his later years he promoted <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_disarmament" title="Nuclear disarmament">nuclear disarmament</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Orthomolecular_medicine" title="Orthomolecular medicine">orthomolecular medicine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Megavitamin_therapy" title="Megavitamin therapy">megavitamin therapy</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-isbn0-399-50764-7_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-isbn0-399-50764-7-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Dietary_supplements" class="mw-redirect" title="Dietary supplements">dietary supplements</a>. None of the latter have gained much acceptance in the mainstream scientific community.<sup id="cite_ref-Horgan_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horgan-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Offit_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Offit-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div id="toc" class="toc" role="navigation" aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_education">Early life and education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linus_Pauling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life and education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Herman_Henry_William_Pauling.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Herman_Henry_William_Pauling.jpg/220px-Herman_Henry_William_Pauling.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="280" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Herman_Henry_William_Pauling.jpg/330px-Herman_Henry_William_Pauling.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Herman_Henry_William_Pauling.jpg/440px-Herman_Henry_William_Pauling.jpg 2x" data-file-width="549" data-file-height="699" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Herman_Henry_William_Pauling.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Herman Henry William Pauling, Linus Pauling's father, <a href="/wiki/Circa" title="Circa">circa</a> 1900</div></div></div>
<p>Linus Carl Pauling was born on February 28, 1901, in <a href="/wiki/Portland,_Oregon" title="Portland, Oregon">Portland, Oregon</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-frs_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frs-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-childhood_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-childhood-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> the firstborn child of Herman Henry William Pauling (1876–1910) and Lucy Isabelle "Belle" Darling (1881–1926).<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> He was named "Linus Carl", in honor of Lucy's father, Linus, and Herman's father, Carl.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> His father was German while his mother was English.
</p><p>In 1902, after his sister Pauline was born, Pauling's parents decided to move out of Portland, to find more affordable and spacious living quarters than their one-room apartment.<sup id="cite_ref-Goertzelp4_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goertzelp4-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Lucy stayed with her husband's parents in <a href="/wiki/Lake_Oswego,_Oregon" title="Lake Oswego, Oregon">Lake Oswego</a> until Herman brought the family to <a href="/wiki/Salem,_Oregon" title="Salem, Oregon">Salem</a>, where he worked briefly as a traveling salesman for the Skidmore Drug Company. Within a year of Lucile's birth in 1904, Herman Pauling moved his family to Lake Oswego, where he opened his own drugstore.<sup id="cite_ref-Goertzelp4_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goertzelp4-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> He moved his family to <a href="/wiki/Condon,_Oregon" title="Condon, Oregon">Condon, Oregon</a>, in 1905.<sup id="cite_ref-Goertzel_and_Goertzel,_p._5_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goertzel_and_Goertzel,_p._5-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> By 1906, Herman Pauling was suffering from recurrent <a href="/wiki/Abdominal_pain" title="Abdominal pain">abdominal pain</a>. He died of a perforated <a href="/wiki/Peptic_ulcer" class="mw-redirect" title="Peptic ulcer">ulcer</a> on June 11, 1910, leaving Lucy to care for Linus, Lucile and Pauline.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Pauling attributes his interest in becoming a chemist to being amazed by experiments conducted by a friend, <a href="/wiki/Lloyd_A._Jeffress" title="Lloyd A. Jeffress">Lloyd A. Jeffress</a>, who had a small chemistry lab kit.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> He later wrote: "I was simply entranced by chemical phenomena, by the reactions in which substances, often with strikingly different properties, appear; and I hoped to learn more and more about this aspect of the world."<sup id="cite_ref-Abrams_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abrams-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In high school, Pauling conducted chemistry experiments by scavenging equipment and material from an abandoned steel plant. With an older friend, Lloyd Simon, Pauling set up Palmon Laboratories in Simon's basement.  They approached local dairies offering to perform butterfat samplings at cheap prices but dairymen were wary of trusting two boys with the task, and the business ended in failure.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At age 15, the high school senior had enough credits to enter <a href="/wiki/Oregon_State_University" title="Oregon State University">Oregon State University</a> (OSU), known then as Oregon Agricultural College.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Lacking two American history courses required for his <a href="/wiki/High_school_diploma" title="High school diploma">high school diploma</a>, Pauling asked the school principal if he could take the courses concurrently during the spring semester. Denied, he left <a href="/wiki/Washington_High_School_(Portland,_Oregon)" class="mw-redirect" title="Washington High School (Portland, Oregon)">Washington High School</a> in June without a diploma.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The school awarded him an honorary diploma 45 years later, after he was awarded two Nobel Prizes.<sup id="cite_ref-Nobel_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nobel-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Pauling held a number of jobs to earn money for his future college expenses, including working part-time at a grocery store for $8 per week (equivalent to $190&#32;in 2019). His mother arranged an interview with the owner of a number of manufacturing plants in Portland, Mr. Schwietzerhoff, who hired him as an apprentice machinist at a salary of $40 per month (equivalent to $940&#32;in 2019). This was soon raised to $50 per month.<sup id="cite_ref-Goertzelp23_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goertzelp23-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Pauling also set up a photography laboratory with two friends.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> In September 1917, Pauling was finally admitted by Oregon State University. He immediately resigned from the machinist's job and informed his mother, who saw no point in a university education, of his plans.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Higher_education">Higher education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linus_Pauling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Higher education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:LinusPaulingGraduation1922.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/LinusPaulingGraduation1922.jpg/220px-LinusPaulingGraduation1922.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="309" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/LinusPaulingGraduation1922.jpg/330px-LinusPaulingGraduation1922.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/LinusPaulingGraduation1922.jpg/440px-LinusPaulingGraduation1922.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1779" data-file-height="2497" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:LinusPaulingGraduation1922.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pauling's graduation photo from <a href="/wiki/Oregon_State_University" title="Oregon State University">Oregon State University</a>, 1922</div></div></div>
<p>In his first semester, Pauling registered for two courses in chemistry, two in mathematics, mechanical drawing, introduction to mining and use of explosives, modern English prose, gymnastics and military drill.<sup id="cite_ref-Goertzelp26_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goertzelp26-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> He was active in campus life and founded the school's chapter of the <a href="/wiki/Delta_Upsilon" title="Delta Upsilon">Delta Upsilon</a> fraternity.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> After his second year, he planned to take a job in Portland to help support his mother. The college offered him a position teaching <a href="/wiki/Quantitative_analysis_(chemistry)" title="Quantitative analysis (chemistry)">quantitative analysis</a>, a course he had just finished taking himself. He worked forty hours a week in the laboratory and classroom and earned $100 a month (equivalent to $1,300&#32;in 2019), enabling him to continue his studies.<sup id="cite_ref-Goertzelp29_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goertzelp29-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In his last two years at school, Pauling became aware of the work of <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_N._Lewis" title="Gilbert N. Lewis">Gilbert N. Lewis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Irving_Langmuir" title="Irving Langmuir">Irving Langmuir</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Electronic_structure" title="Electronic structure">electronic structure</a> of atoms and their <a href="/wiki/Chemical_bond" title="Chemical bond">bonding</a> to form <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecules</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Goertzelp29_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goertzelp29-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> He decided to focus his research on how the <a href="/wiki/Physical_property" title="Physical property">physical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chemical_property" title="Chemical property">chemical properties</a> of substances are related to the structure of the atoms of which they are composed, becoming one of the founders of the new science of quantum chemistry.
</p><p>Engineering professor Samuel Graf selected Pauling to be his teaching assistant in a mechanics and materials course.<sup id="cite_ref-OSUArchives_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OSUArchives-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marinacci_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marinacci-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> During the winter of his senior year, Pauling taught a chemistry course for <a href="/wiki/Family_and_consumer_science" class="mw-redirect" title="Family and consumer science">home economics</a> majors. It was in one of these classes that Pauling met his future wife, <a href="/wiki/Ava_Helen_Pauling" title="Ava Helen Pauling">Ava Helen Miller</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Marinacci_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marinacci-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>41</span></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AvaBook_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AvaBook-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1922, Pauling graduated from <a href="/wiki/Oregon_State_University" title="Oregon State University">Oregon State University</a><sup id="cite_ref-frs_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frs-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> (known then as Oregon Agricultural College) with a degree in <a href="/wiki/Chemical_engineering" title="Chemical engineering">chemical engineering</a>. He went on to <a href="/wiki/Graduate_school" title="Graduate school">graduate school</a> at the <a href="/wiki/California_Institute_of_Technology" title="California Institute of Technology">California Institute of Technology</a> (Caltech) in <a href="/wiki/Pasadena,_California" title="Pasadena, California">Pasadena, California</a>, under the guidance of <a href="/wiki/Roscoe_G._Dickinson" title="Roscoe G. Dickinson">Roscoe Dickinson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Richard_C._Tolman" title="Richard C. Tolman">Richard Tolman</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-mathgene_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mathgene-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> His graduate research involved the use of <a href="/wiki/X-ray_diffraction" class="mw-redirect" title="X-ray diffraction">X-ray diffraction</a> to determine the structure of <a href="/wiki/Crystal" title="Crystal">crystals</a>. He published seven papers on the <a href="/wiki/Crystal_structure" title="Crystal structure">crystal structure</a> of minerals while he was at Caltech. He received his PhD in <a href="/wiki/Physical_chemistry" title="Physical chemistry">physical chemistry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_physics" title="Mathematical physics">mathematical physics</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-paulingphd_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-paulingphd-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Summa_cum_laude" class="mw-redirect" title="Summa cum laude">summa cum laude</a>, in 1925.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Career">Career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linus_Pauling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="infobox" style="width:22em;width: 190px; float: right; clear: right; margin:0 0 1.5em 1.5em"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-size:125%;font-weight:bold;font-size:115%">External video</th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a href="/wiki/File:Sickle_Cell_Blood_Smear.JPG" class="image"><img alt="Sickle Cell Blood Smear.JPG" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Sickle_Cell_Blood_Smear.JPG/170px-Sickle_Cell_Blood_Smear.JPG" decoding="async" width="170" height="128" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Sickle_Cell_Blood_Smear.JPG/255px-Sickle_Cell_Blood_Smear.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Sickle_Cell_Blood_Smear.JPG/340px-Sickle_Cell_Blood_Smear.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="960" /></a></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;text-align: left"><img alt="video icon" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/16px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png" decoding="async" width="16" height="16" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/24px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/32px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="128" data-file-height="128" /> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://watch.opb.org/video/1954582651/"><i>Linus Pauling</i></a>, Oregon Experience, <a href="/wiki/Oregon_Historical_Society" title="Oregon Historical Society">Oregon Historical Society</a></td></tr></tbody></table><p>In 1926, Pauling was awarded a <a href="/wiki/Guggenheim_Fellowship" title="Guggenheim Fellowship">Guggenheim Fellowship</a> to travel to Europe, to study under German physicist <a href="/wiki/Arnold_Sommerfeld" title="Arnold Sommerfeld">Arnold Sommerfeld</a> in Munich, Danish physicist <a href="/wiki/Niels_Bohr" title="Niels Bohr">Niels Bohr</a> in Copenhagen and Austrian physicist <a href="/wiki/Erwin_Schr%C3%B6dinger" title="Erwin Schrödinger">Erwin Schrödinger</a> in <a href="/wiki/Z%C3%BCrich" title="Zürich">Zürich</a>. All three were experts in the new field of quantum mechanics and other branches of physics.<sup id="cite_ref-Guggenheim_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guggenheim-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Pauling became interested in how quantum mechanics might be applied in his chosen field of interest, the <a href="/wiki/Electron_configuration" title="Electron configuration">electronic structure</a> of atoms and molecules. In Zürich, Pauling was also exposed to one of the first quantum mechanical analyses of bonding in the <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> molecule, done by <a href="/wiki/Walter_Heitler" title="Walter Heitler">Walter Heitler</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fritz_London" title="Fritz London">Fritz London</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Realism_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Realism-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Pauling devoted the two years of his European trip to this work and decided to make it the focus of his future research. He became one of the first scientists in the field of quantum chemistry and a pioneer in the application of quantum theory to the structure of molecules.<sup id="cite_ref-PNNL_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PNNL-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1927, Pauling took a new position as an assistant professor at <a href="/wiki/Caltech" class="mw-redirect" title="Caltech">Caltech</a> in <a href="/wiki/Theoretical_chemistry" title="Theoretical chemistry">theoretical chemistry</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-OH_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OH-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> He launched his faculty career with a very productive five years, continuing with his <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-ray</a> crystal studies and also performing quantum mechanical calculations on atoms and molecules. He published approximately fifty papers in those five years, and created the five rules now known as <a href="/wiki/Pauling%27s_rules" title="Pauling&#39;s rules">Pauling's rules</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pauling1960_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pauling1960-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> By 1929, he was promoted to associate professor, and by 1930, to full professor.<sup id="cite_ref-OH_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OH-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> In 1931, the <a href="/wiki/American_Chemical_Society" title="American Chemical Society">American Chemical Society</a> awarded Pauling the Langmuir Prize for the most significant work in pure science by a person 30 years of age or younger.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> The following year, Pauling published what he regarded as his most important paper, in which he first laid out the concept of <a href="/wiki/Orbital_hybridization" class="mw-redirect" title="Orbital hybridization">hybridization of atomic orbitals</a> and analyzed the <a href="/wiki/Tetravalence" class="mw-redirect" title="Tetravalence">tetravalency</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> atom.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At Caltech, Pauling struck up a close friendship with <a href="/wiki/Theoretical_physics" title="Theoretical physics">theoretical physicist</a> <a href="/wiki/Robert_Oppenheimer" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert Oppenheimer">Robert Oppenheimer</a>, who spent part of his research and teaching schedule away from <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_Berkeley" title="University of California, Berkeley">U.C. Berkeley</a> at Caltech every year.<sup id="cite_ref-Nature_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nature-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Oppenheimer_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oppenheimer-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Pauling was also affiliated to UC Berkeley as Visiting Lecturer in Physics and Chemistry from 1929 to 1934.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Oppenheimer even gave Pauling a stunning personal collection of minerals.<sup id="cite_ref-LostAlly_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LostAlly-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>  The two men planned to mount a joint attack on the nature of the chemical bond: apparently Oppenheimer would supply the mathematics and Pauling would interpret the results. Their relationship soured when Oppenheimer tried to pursue Pauling's wife, Ava Helen. When Pauling was at work, Oppenheimer came to their home and blurted out an invitation to Ava Helen to join him on a tryst in Mexico. She flatly refused, and reported the incident to Pauling. He immediately cut off his relationship with Oppenheimer.<sup id="cite_ref-Nature_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nature-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>152</span></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Oppenheimer_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oppenheimer-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the summer of 1930, Pauling made another European trip, during which he learned about gas-phase electron <a href="/wiki/Diffraction" title="Diffraction">diffraction</a> from <a href="/wiki/Herman_Francis_Mark" title="Herman Francis Mark">Herman Francis Mark</a>. After returning, he built an <a href="/wiki/Electron_diffraction" title="Electron diffraction">electron diffraction</a> instrument at Caltech with a student of his, Lawrence Olin Brockway, and used it to study the <a href="/wiki/Molecular_geometry" title="Molecular geometry">molecular structure</a> of a large number of chemical substances.<sup id="cite_ref-Hargittai_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hargittai-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Pauling introduced the concept of <a href="/wiki/Electronegativity" title="Electronegativity">electronegativity</a> in 1932.<sup id="cite_ref-paulingJACS_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-paulingJACS-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Using the various properties of molecules, such as the energy required to break bonds and the <a href="/wiki/Dipole" title="Dipole">dipole</a> <a href="/wiki/Moment_(physics)" title="Moment (physics)">moments</a> of molecules, he established a scale and an associated numerical value for most of the elements – the <a href="/wiki/Pauling_scale_of_electronegativity" class="mw-redirect" title="Pauling scale of electronegativity">Pauling Electronegativity Scale</a> – which is useful in predicting the nature of bonds between atoms in molecules.<sup id="cite_ref-Scale2_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scale2-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1936, Pauling was promoted to Chairman of the Division of Chemistry and Chemical Engineering at Caltech, and to the position of Director of the Gates and Crellin laboratories of Chemistry. He would hold both positions until 1958.<sup id="cite_ref-OH_43-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OH-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Pauling also spent a year in 1948 at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Oxford" title="University of Oxford">University of Oxford</a> as George Eastman Visiting Professor and Fellow of Balliol.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nature_of_the_chemical_bond">Nature of the chemical bond</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linus_Pauling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Nature of the chemical bond">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Linus_Pauling_1955a.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Linus_Pauling_1955a.jpg/220px-Linus_Pauling_1955a.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="254" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Linus_Pauling_1955a.jpg/330px-Linus_Pauling_1955a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Linus_Pauling_1955a.jpg/440px-Linus_Pauling_1955a.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1431" data-file-height="1655" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Linus_Pauling_1955a.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Linus Pauling with an inset of his Nobel Prize in 1955</div></div></div>
<p>In the late 1920s, Pauling began publishing papers on the nature of the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_bond" title="Chemical bond">chemical bond</a>. Between 1937 and 1938, he took a position as George Fischer Baker Non-Resident Lecturer in Chemistry at <a href="/wiki/Cornell_University" title="Cornell University">Cornell University</a>. While at Cornell, he delivered a series of nineteen lectures<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> and completed the bulk of his famous textbook <i>The Nature of the Chemical Bond</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-The_George_Fischer_Baker_Lectureship_and_the_Beginnings_of_the_Manuscript_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_George_Fischer_Baker_Lectureship_and_the_Beginnings_of_the_Manuscript-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pauling_Book_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pauling_Book-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>Preface</span></sup> It is based primarily on his work in this area that he received the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Chemistry" title="Nobel Prize in Chemistry">Nobel Prize in Chemistry</a> in 1954 "for his research into the nature of the chemical bond and its application to the elucidation of the structure of complex substances".<sup id="cite_ref-Nobel_8-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nobel-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Pauling's book has been considered "chemistry's most influential book of this century and its effective bible".<sup id="cite_ref-Genes2001_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Genes2001-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> In the 30 years after its first edition was published in 1939, the book was cited more than 16,000 times. Even today, many modern scientific papers and articles in important journals cite this work, more than seventy years after the first publication.<sup id="cite_ref-Google_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Google-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Part of Pauling's work on the nature of the chemical bond led to his introduction of the concept of <a href="/wiki/Orbital_hybridization" class="mw-redirect" title="Orbital hybridization">orbital hybridization</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> While it is normal to think of the electrons in an atom as being described by <a href="/wiki/Atomic_orbital" title="Atomic orbital">orbitals</a> of types such as <i>s</i> and <i>p</i>, it turns out that in describing the bonding in molecules, it is better to construct functions that partake of some of the properties of each. Thus the one 2s and three 2p orbitals in a carbon atom can be (mathematically) 'mixed' or combined to make four equivalent orbitals (called sp<sup>3</sup> hybrid orbitals), which would be the appropriate orbitals to describe carbon compounds such as <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, or the 2s orbital may be combined with two of the 2p orbitals to make three equivalent orbitals (called sp<sup>2</sup> hybrid orbitals), with the remaining 2p orbital unhybridized, which would be the appropriate orbitals to describe certain <a href="/wiki/Alkene" title="Alkene">unsaturated</a> carbon compounds such as <a href="/wiki/Ethylene" title="Ethylene">ethylene</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Pauling_Book_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pauling_Book-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>111–120</span></sup> Other hybridization schemes are also found in other types of molecules.
Another area which he explored was the relationship between <a href="/wiki/Ionic_bond" class="mw-redirect" title="Ionic bond">ionic bonding</a>, where electrons are transferred between atoms, and <a href="/wiki/Covalent_bond" title="Covalent bond">covalent bonding</a>, where electrons are shared between atoms on an equal basis. Pauling showed that these were merely extremes, and that for most actual cases of bonding, the <a href="/wiki/Introduction_to_quantum_mechanics" title="Introduction to quantum mechanics">quantum-mechanical</a> <a href="/wiki/Wave_function" title="Wave function">wave function</a> for a polar molecule AB is a <a href="/wiki/Linear_combination" title="Linear combination">combination</a> of wave functions for covalent and ionic molecules.<sup id="cite_ref-Pauling1960_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pauling1960-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>66</span></sup> Here Pauling's <i><a href="/wiki/Electronegativity" title="Electronegativity">electronegativity</a></i> concept is particularly useful; the electronegativity difference between a pair of atoms will be the surest predictor of the degree of ionicity of the bond.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The third of the topics that Pauling attacked under the overall heading of "the nature of the chemical bond" was the accounting of the structure of <a href="/wiki/Aromatic_hydrocarbon" class="mw-redirect" title="Aromatic hydrocarbon">aromatic hydrocarbons</a>, particularly the prototype, <a href="/wiki/Benzene" title="Benzene">benzene</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> The best description of benzene had been made by the German chemist <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_August_Kekul%C3%A9_von_Stradonitz" class="mw-redirect" title="Friedrich August Kekulé von Stradonitz">Friedrich Kekulé</a>. He had treated it as a rapid interconversion between two structures, each with alternating single and <a href="/wiki/Double_bond" title="Double bond">double bonds</a>, but with the double bonds of one structure in the locations where the single bonds were in the other. Pauling showed that a proper description based on quantum mechanics was an intermediate structure which was a blend of each. The structure was a superposition of structures rather than a rapid interconversion between them. The name "<a href="/wiki/Resonance_(chemistry)" title="Resonance (chemistry)">resonance</a>" was later applied to this phenomenon.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> In a sense, this phenomenon resembles those of hybridization and also polar bonding, both described above, because all three phenomena involve combining more than one electronic structure to achieve an intermediate result.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ionic_crystal_structures">Ionic crystal structures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linus_Pauling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Ionic crystal structures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1929, Pauling published <a href="/wiki/Pauling%27s_rules" title="Pauling&#39;s rules">five rules</a> which help to <a href="/wiki/Crystal_structure_prediction" title="Crystal structure prediction">predict</a> and explain <a href="/wiki/Crystal_structure" title="Crystal structure">crystal structures</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ionic_compound" title="Ionic compound">ionic compounds</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pauling1960_45-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pauling1960-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> 
These rules concern (1) the ratio of cation radius to anion radius, (2) the electrostatic bond strength, (3) the sharing of polyhedron corners, edges and faces, (4) crystals containing different cations, and (5) the rule of parsimony.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biological_molecules">Biological molecules</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linus_Pauling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Biological molecules">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Linus_Pauling_1941.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Linus_Pauling_1941.png/220px-Linus_Pauling_1941.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="300" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Linus_Pauling_1941.png/330px-Linus_Pauling_1941.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Linus_Pauling_1941.png/440px-Linus_Pauling_1941.png 2x" data-file-width="601" data-file-height="820" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Linus_Pauling_1941.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pauling in 1941</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Helix_electron_density_myoglobin_2nrl_17-32.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Helix_electron_density_myoglobin_2nrl_17-32.jpg/170px-Helix_electron_density_myoglobin_2nrl_17-32.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="299" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Helix_electron_density_myoglobin_2nrl_17-32.jpg/255px-Helix_electron_density_myoglobin_2nrl_17-32.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Helix_electron_density_myoglobin_2nrl_17-32.jpg/340px-Helix_electron_density_myoglobin_2nrl_17-32.jpg 2x" data-file-width="740" data-file-height="1300" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Helix_electron_density_myoglobin_2nrl_17-32.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An alpha helix in ultra-high-resolution electron density contours, with O atoms in red, N atoms in blue, and hydrogen bonds as green dotted lines (PDB file 2NRL, 17-32).</div></div></div>
<p>In the mid-1930s, Pauling, strongly influenced by the biologically oriented funding priorities of the Rockefeller Foundation's <a href="/wiki/Warren_Weaver" title="Warren Weaver">Warren Weaver</a>, decided to strike out into new areas of interest.<sup id="cite_ref-Kay_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kay-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Although Pauling's early interest had focused almost exclusively on inorganic molecular structures, he had occasionally thought about molecules of biological importance, in part because of Caltech's growing strength in biology. Pauling interacted with such great biologists as <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hunt_Morgan" title="Thomas Hunt Morgan">Thomas Hunt Morgan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Theodosius_Dobzhanski" class="mw-redirect" title="Theodosius Dobzhanski">Theodosius Dobzhanski</a>, <a href="/wiki/Calvin_Bridges" title="Calvin Bridges">Calvin Bridges</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Sturtevant" title="Alfred Sturtevant">Alfred Sturtevant</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Califano_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Califano-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> His early work in this area included studies of the structure of <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">hemoglobin</a> with his student <a href="/wiki/Charles_D._Coryell" title="Charles D. Coryell">Charles D. Coryell</a>. He demonstrated that the hemoglobin molecule changes structure when it gains or loses an <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> atom.<sup id="cite_ref-Califano_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Califano-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> As a result of this observation, he decided to conduct a more thorough study of <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a> structure in general. He returned to his earlier use of X-ray diffraction analysis. But protein structures were far less amenable to this technique than the crystalline minerals of his former work. The best X-ray pictures of proteins in the 1930s had been made by the British crystallographer <a href="/wiki/William_Astbury" title="William Astbury">William Astbury</a>, but when Pauling tried, in 1937, to account for Astbury's observations quantum mechanically, he could not.<sup id="cite_ref-Livio_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Livio-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It took eleven years for Pauling to explain the problem: his <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematical</a> analysis was correct, but Astbury's pictures were taken in such a way that the protein molecules were tilted from their expected positions. Pauling had formulated a model for the structure of hemoglobin in which atoms were arranged in a <a href="/wiki/Helix" title="Helix">helical</a> pattern, and applied this idea to proteins in general.
</p><p>In 1951, based on the structures of <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peptide" title="Peptide">peptides</a> and the planar nature of the peptide bond, Pauling, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Corey" title="Robert Corey">Robert Corey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Herman_Branson" title="Herman Branson">Herman Branson</a> correctly proposed the <a href="/wiki/Alpha_helix" title="Alpha helix">alpha helix</a> and <a href="/wiki/Beta_sheet" title="Beta sheet">beta sheet</a> as the primary structural motifs in protein <a href="/wiki/Secondary_structure" class="mw-redirect" title="Secondary structure">secondary structure</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Goertzel_and_Goertzel,_p._95-100_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goertzel_and_Goertzel,_p._95-100-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> This work exemplified Pauling's ability to think unconventionally; central to the structure was the unorthodox assumption that one turn of the helix may well contain a non-<a href="/wiki/Integer" title="Integer">integer</a> number of amino acid residues; for the alpha helix it is 3.7 amino acid residues per turn.
</p><p>Pauling then proposed that <a href="/wiki/Deoxyribonucleic_acid" class="mw-redirect" title="Deoxyribonucleic acid">deoxyribonucleic acid</a> (DNA) was a <a href="/wiki/Triple_helix" title="Triple helix">triple helix</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> his model contained several basic mistakes, including a proposal of neutral phosphate groups, an idea that conflicted with the acidity of DNA. <a href="/wiki/William_Lawrence_Bragg" class="mw-redirect" title="William Lawrence Bragg">Sir Lawrence Bragg</a> had been disappointed that Pauling had won the race to find the alpha helix structure of proteins. Bragg's team had made a fundamental error in making their models of protein by not recognizing the planar nature of the peptide bond. When it was learned at the <a href="/wiki/Cavendish_Laboratory" title="Cavendish Laboratory">Cavendish Laboratory</a> that Pauling was working on molecular models of the structure of DNA, <a href="/wiki/James_Watson" title="James Watson">James Watson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Francis_Crick" title="Francis Crick">Francis Crick</a> were allowed to make a molecular model of DNA. They later benefited from unpublished data from <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Wilkins" title="Maurice Wilkins">Maurice Wilkins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rosalind_Franklin" title="Rosalind Franklin">Rosalind Franklin</a> at <a href="/wiki/King%27s_College_London" title="King&#39;s College London">King's College</a> which showed evidence for a helix and planar base stacking along the helix axis. Early in 1953 Watson and Crick proposed a correct structure for the DNA double helix. Pauling later cited several reasons to explain how he had been misled about the structure of DNA, among them misleading density data and the lack of high quality X-ray diffraction photographs. During the time Pauling was researching the problem, Rosalind Franklin in England was creating the world's best images. They were key to Watson's and Crick's success. Pauling did not see them before devising his mistaken DNA structure, although his assistant Robert Corey did see at least some of them, while taking Pauling's place at a summer 1952 protein conference in England. Pauling had been prevented from attending because his passport was withheld by the State Department on suspicion that he had Communist sympathies. This led to the legend that Pauling missed the structure of DNA because of the politics of the day (this was at the start of the <a href="/wiki/McCarthyism" title="McCarthyism">McCarthy</a> period in the United States). Politics did not play a critical role. Not only did Corey see the images at the time, but Pauling himself regained his passport within a few weeks and toured English laboratories well before writing his DNA paper. He had ample opportunity to visit Franklin's lab and see her work, but chose not to.<sup id="cite_ref-Nature_48-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nature-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>414–415</span></sup>
</p><p>Pauling also studied <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzyme</a> reactions and was among the first to point out that enzymes bring about reactions by stabilizing the <a href="/wiki/Transition_state" title="Transition state">transition state</a> of the reaction, a view which is central to understanding their mechanism of action.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> He was also among the first scientists to postulate that the binding of <a href="/wiki/Antibodies" class="mw-redirect" title="Antibodies">antibodies</a> to antigens would be due to a complementarity between their structures.<sup id="cite_ref-Lewis_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Along the same lines, with the physicist turned biologist <a href="/wiki/Max_Delbr%C3%BCck" title="Max Delbrück">Max Delbrück</a>, he wrote an early paper arguing that <a href="/wiki/DNA_replication" title="DNA replication">DNA replication</a> was likely to be due to <a href="/wiki/Complementarity_(molecular_biology)" title="Complementarity (molecular biology)">complementarity</a>, rather than similarity, as suggested by a few researchers. This was made clear in the model of the structure of DNA that Watson and Crick discovered.<sup id="cite_ref-Tudge_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tudge-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Molecular_genetics">Molecular genetics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linus_Pauling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Molecular genetics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Linus_Pauling_1948.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Linus_Pauling_1948.png/220px-Linus_Pauling_1948.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="309" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Linus_Pauling_1948.png/330px-Linus_Pauling_1948.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Linus_Pauling_1948.png/440px-Linus_Pauling_1948.png 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="900" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Linus_Pauling_1948.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pauling in 1948</div></div></div>
<p>In November 1949, Pauling, <a href="/wiki/Harvey_Itano" title="Harvey Itano">Harvey Itano</a>, <a href="/wiki/S._J._Singer" class="mw-redirect" title="S. J. Singer">S. J. Singer</a> and Ibert Wells published "<a href="/wiki/Sickle_Cell_Anemia,_a_Molecular_Disease" title="Sickle Cell Anemia, a Molecular Disease">Sickle Cell Anemia, a Molecular Disease</a>"<sup id="cite_ref-Itano_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Itano-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> in the journal <i>Science</i>. It was the first proof of a human disease caused by an abnormal protein, and sickle cell anemia became the first disease understood at the molecular level. Using <a href="/wiki/Electrophoresis" title="Electrophoresis">electrophoresis</a>, they demonstrated that individuals with <a href="/wiki/Sickle_cell_disease" title="Sickle cell disease">sickle cell disease</a> have a modified form of hemoglobin in their <a href="/wiki/Red_blood_cell" title="Red blood cell">red blood cells</a>, and that individuals with <a href="/wiki/Sickle_cell_trait" title="Sickle cell trait">sickle cell trait</a> have both the normal and abnormal forms of hemoglobin. This was the first demonstration causally linking an abnormal protein to a disease, and also the first demonstration that <a href="/wiki/Mendelian_inheritance" title="Mendelian inheritance">Mendelian inheritance</a> determines the specific physical properties of proteins, not simply their presence or absence – the dawn of <a href="/wiki/Molecular_genetics" title="Molecular genetics">molecular genetics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Strasser_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Strasser-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>His success with sickle cell anemia led Pauling to speculate that a number of other diseases, including mental illnesses such as schizophrenia, might result from flawed genetics. As chairman of the Division of Chemistry and Chemical Engineering and director of the Gates and Crellin Chemical Laboratories, he encouraged the hiring of researchers with a chemical-biomedical approach to mental illness, a direction not always popular with established Caltech chemists.<sup id="cite_ref-LATimes1994_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LATimes1994-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>2</span></sup>
</p><p>In 1951, Pauling gave a lecture entitled "Molecular Medicine".<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> In the late 1950s, Pauling studied the role of enzymes in brain function, believing that mental illness may be partly caused by enzyme dysfunction.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure_of_the_atomic_nucleus">Structure of the atomic nucleus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linus_Pauling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Structure of the atomic nucleus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On September 16, 1952, Pauling opened a new research notebook with the words "I have decided to attack the problem of the structure of nuclei." On October 15, 1965, Pauling published his Close-Packed Spheron Model of the atomic nucleus in two well respected journals, <i>Science</i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Proceedings_of_the_National_Academy_of_Sciences" class="mw-redirect" title="Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences">Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-SpheronPNAS_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SpheronPNAS-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SpheronScience_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SpheronScience-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> For nearly three decades, until his death in 1994, Pauling published numerous papers on his spheron cluster model.<sup id="cite_ref-SpheronPNAS_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SpheronPNAS-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The basic idea behind Pauling's spheron model is that a nucleus can be viewed as a set of "clusters of nucleons". The basic nucleon clusters include the <a href="/wiki/Deuteron" class="mw-redirect" title="Deuteron">deuteron</a> [np], <a href="/wiki/Helion_(chemistry)" title="Helion (chemistry)">helion</a> [pnp], and <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">triton</a> [npn]. Even–even nuclei are described as being composed of clusters of <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">alpha particles</a>, as has often been done for light nuclei.<sup id="cite_ref-Clusters_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clusters-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> Pauling attempted to derive the shell structure of nuclei from pure geometrical considerations related to <a href="/wiki/Platonic_solids" class="mw-redirect" title="Platonic solids">Platonic solids</a> rather than starting from an independent particle model as in the usual <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_shell_model" title="Nuclear shell model">shell model</a>. In an interview given in 1990 Pauling commented on his model:<sup id="cite_ref-AOA_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AOA-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r960796168">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Now recently, I have been trying to determine detailed structures of atomic nuclei by analyzing the ground state and excited state vibrational bends, as observed experimentally. From reading the physics literature, Physical Review Letters and other journals, I know that many physicists are interested in atomic nuclei, but none of them, so far as I have been able to discover, has been attacking the problem in the same way that I attack it. So I just move along at my own speed, making calculations ...</p></blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Activism">Activism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linus_Pauling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Activism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wartime_work">Wartime work</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linus_Pauling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Wartime work">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Beckman_D2_Oxygen_Analyzer_2012_002_5132_cn69m4659.tiff" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Beckman_D2_Oxygen_Analyzer_2012_002_5132_cn69m4659.tiff/lossless-page1-220px-Beckman_D2_Oxygen_Analyzer_2012_002_5132_cn69m4659.tiff.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Beckman_D2_Oxygen_Analyzer_2012_002_5132_cn69m4659.tiff/lossless-page1-330px-Beckman_D2_Oxygen_Analyzer_2012_002_5132_cn69m4659.tiff.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Beckman_D2_Oxygen_Analyzer_2012_002_5132_cn69m4659.tiff/lossless-page1-440px-Beckman_D2_Oxygen_Analyzer_2012_002_5132_cn69m4659.tiff.png 2x" data-file-width="1042" data-file-height="734" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Beckman_D2_Oxygen_Analyzer_2012_002_5132_cn69m4659.tiff" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Beckman D2 Oxygen Analyzer, ca.1950</div></div></div>
<p>Pauling had been practically apolitical until <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>. At the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Project" title="Manhattan Project">Manhattan Project</a>, Robert Oppenheimer invited him to be in charge of the Chemistry division of the project. However, he declined, not wanting to uproot his family.<sup id="cite_ref-Hiroshima_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hiroshima-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Beckman_Model_735_Dissolved_O2_Analyzer_2012_002_3598_jm214p459_crop.tiff" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Beckman_Model_735_Dissolved_O2_Analyzer_2012_002_3598_jm214p459_crop.tiff/lossless-page1-220px-Beckman_Model_735_Dissolved_O2_Analyzer_2012_002_3598_jm214p459_crop.tiff.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="195" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Beckman_Model_735_Dissolved_O2_Analyzer_2012_002_3598_jm214p459_crop.tiff/lossless-page1-330px-Beckman_Model_735_Dissolved_O2_Analyzer_2012_002_3598_jm214p459_crop.tiff.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Beckman_Model_735_Dissolved_O2_Analyzer_2012_002_3598_jm214p459_crop.tiff/lossless-page1-440px-Beckman_Model_735_Dissolved_O2_Analyzer_2012_002_3598_jm214p459_crop.tiff.png 2x" data-file-width="1118" data-file-height="989" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Beckman_Model_735_Dissolved_O2_Analyzer_2012_002_3598_jm214p459_crop.tiff" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Beckman Model 735 Dissolved O2 Analyzer, later model based on Pauling's design, 1968</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Beckman_Model_D_Oxygen_Meter_with_infant_incubator_73666474j.tiff" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Beckman_Model_D_Oxygen_Meter_with_infant_incubator_73666474j.tiff/lossless-page1-220px-Beckman_Model_D_Oxygen_Meter_with_infant_incubator_73666474j.tiff.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Beckman_Model_D_Oxygen_Meter_with_infant_incubator_73666474j.tiff/lossless-page1-330px-Beckman_Model_D_Oxygen_Meter_with_infant_incubator_73666474j.tiff.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Beckman_Model_D_Oxygen_Meter_with_infant_incubator_73666474j.tiff/lossless-page1-440px-Beckman_Model_D_Oxygen_Meter_with_infant_incubator_73666474j.tiff.png 2x" data-file-width="2951" data-file-height="2360" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Beckman_Model_D_Oxygen_Meter_with_infant_incubator_73666474j.tiff" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Beckman Model D Oxygen Meter, based on Pauling's design, with infant incubator, 1959</div></div></div>
<p>Pauling did, however, work on research for the military. He was a principal investigator on 14 <a href="/wiki/Office_of_Scientific_Research_and_Development" title="Office of Scientific Research and Development">OSRD contracts</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Kay2_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kay2-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>  The <a href="/wiki/National_Defense_Research_Committee" title="National Defense Research Committee">National Defense Research Committee</a> called a meeting on October 3, 1940, wanting an instrument that could reliably measure oxygen content in a mixture of gases, so that they could measure oxygen conditions in submarines and airplanes. In response Pauling designed the Pauling oxygen meter, which was developed and manufactured by <a href="/wiki/Arnold_O._Beckman" class="mw-redirect" title="Arnold O. Beckman">Arnold O. Beckman, Inc.</a> After the war, Beckman adapted the oxygen analyzers for use in incubators for premature babies.<sup id="cite_ref-hundred_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hundred-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>180–186</span></sup><sup id="cite_ref-D2OxygenAnalyzer_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-D2OxygenAnalyzer-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1942, Pauling successfully submitted a proposal on "The Chemical Treatment of Protein Solutions in the Attempt to Find a Substitute for Human Serum for Transfusions". His project group, which included J.B. Koepfli and Dan Campbell, developed a possible replacement for <a href="/wiki/Blood_plasma" title="Blood plasma">human blood plasma</a> in <a href="/wiki/Blood_transfusion" title="Blood transfusion">transfusions</a>: <a href="/w/index.php?title=Polyoxy_gelatin&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Polyoxy gelatin (page does not exist)">polyoxy gelatin</a> (Oxypolygelatin).<sup id="cite_ref-Oxypolygelatin_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oxypolygelatin-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Chadarevian_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chadarevian-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Other wartime projects with more direct military applications included work on explosives, rocket propellants and the patent for an armor-piercing shell.  In October 1948 Pauling was awarded a <a href="/wiki/Presidential_Medal_for_Merit" class="mw-redirect" title="Presidential Medal for Merit">Presidential Medal for Merit</a> by President <a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman" title="Harry S. Truman">Harry S. Truman</a>.  The citation credits him for his "imaginative mind", "brilliant success", and "exceptionally meritorious conduct in the performance of outstanding services.<sup id="cite_ref-PMFM_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PMFM-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NLM_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NLM-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Paulus_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Paulus-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>  In 1949, he served as president of the <a href="/wiki/American_Chemical_Society" title="American Chemical Society">American Chemical Society</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ACS1949_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ACS1949-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_activism">Nuclear activism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linus_Pauling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Nuclear activism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The aftermath of the <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Project" title="Manhattan Project">Manhattan Project</a> and his wife Ava's pacifism changed Pauling's life profoundly, and he became a peace activist.
</p><p>In June 1945, a "May-Johnson Bill" began<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> that would become the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_Energy_Act_of_1946" title="Atomic Energy Act of 1946">Atomic Energy Act of 1946</a> (signed August 1, 1946). In November 1945, Pauling spoke to the <a href="/wiki/Independent_Citizens_Committee_of_the_Arts,_Sciences_and_Professions" title="Independent Citizens Committee of the Arts, Sciences and Professions">Independent Citizens Committee of the Arts, Sciences and Professions</a> (ICCASP) on <a href="/wiki/Atomic_weapons" class="mw-redirect" title="Atomic weapons">atomic weapons</a>; shortly after, wife Ava and he accepted membership.<sup id="cite_ref-PaulingICCASP_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PaulingICCASP-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>  On January 21, 1946, the group met to discuss <a href="/wiki/Academic_freedom" title="Academic freedom">academic freedom</a>, during which Pauling said, "There is, of course, always a threat to academic freedom – as there is to the other aspects of the freedom and rights of the individual, in the continued attacks which are made on this freedom, these rights, by the selfish, the overly ambitious, the misguided, the unscrupulous, who seek to oppress the great body of mankind in order that they themselves may profit – and we must always be on the alert against this threat, and must fight it with vigor when it becomes dangerous."<sup id="cite_ref-PaulingICCASP_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PaulingICCASP-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1946, he joined the <a href="/wiki/Emergency_Committee_of_Atomic_Scientists" title="Emergency Committee of Atomic Scientists">Emergency Committee of Atomic Scientists</a>, chaired by <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Its mission was to warn the public of the dangers associated with the development of nuclear weapons.
</p><p>His political activism prompted the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_State" title="United States Department of State">US State Department</a> to deny him a passport in 1952, when he was invited to speak at a scientific conference in London.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> In a speech before the <a href="/wiki/US_Senate" class="mw-redirect" title="US Senate">US Senate</a> on June 6 of the same year, Senator <a href="/wiki/Wayne_Morse" title="Wayne Morse">Wayne Morse</a> publicly denounced the action of the State Department, and urged the Passport Division to reverse its decision. Pauling and his wife Ava were then issued a "limited passport" to attend the aforementioned conference in England.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> His full passport was restored in 1954, shortly before the ceremony in <a href="/wiki/Stockholm" title="Stockholm">Stockholm</a> where he received his first Nobel Prize.
</p><p>Joining Einstein, <a href="/wiki/Bertrand_Russell" title="Bertrand Russell">Bertrand Russell</a> and eight other leading scientists and intellectuals, he signed the <a href="/wiki/Russell-Einstein_Manifesto" class="mw-redirect" title="Russell-Einstein Manifesto">Russell-Einstein Manifesto</a> issued July 9, 1955.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> He also supported the <a href="/wiki/Mainau_Declaration" title="Mainau Declaration">Mainau Declaration</a>  of July 15, 1955, signed by 52 Nobel Prize laureates.<sup id="cite_ref-Snow_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Snow-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In May 1957, working with  <a href="/wiki/Washington_University_in_St._Louis" title="Washington University in St. Louis">Washington University in St. Louis</a> professor <a href="/wiki/Barry_Commoner" title="Barry Commoner">Barry Commoner</a>, Pauling began to circulate a petition among scientists to stop nuclear testing.<sup id="cite_ref-Tooth_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tooth-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>  On January 15, 1958, Pauling and his wife presented a petition to United Nations Secretary General <a href="/wiki/Dag_Hammarskj%C3%B6ld" title="Dag Hammarskjöld">Dag Hammarskjöld</a> calling for an end to <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_testing" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear testing">the testing of nuclear weapons</a>.  It was signed by 11,021 scientists representing fifty countries.<sup id="cite_ref-NobelLecture_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NobelLecture-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NobelBlog_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NobelBlog-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In February 1958, Pauling participated in a publicly televised debate with the atomic physicist <a href="/wiki/Edward_Teller" title="Edward Teller">Edward Teller</a> about the actual probability of fallout causing mutations.<sup id="cite_ref-Moore_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moore-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>  Later in 1958, Pauling published <i>No more war!</i>, in which he not only called for an end to the testing of nuclear weapons but also an end to war itself . He proposed that a World Peace Research Organization be set up as part of the United Nations to "attack the problem of preserving the peace".<sup id="cite_ref-Nobel_8-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nobel-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Pauling also supported the work of the St. Louis Citizen's Committee for Nuclear Information (CNI).<sup id="cite_ref-Tooth_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tooth-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> This group, headed by <a href="/wiki/Barry_Commoner" title="Barry Commoner">Barry Commoner</a>, Eric Reiss,  M. W. Friedlander and John Fowler, organized a longitudinal study to measure radioactive <a href="/wiki/Strontium" title="Strontium">strontium</a>-90 in the <a href="/wiki/Baby_teeth" class="mw-redirect" title="Baby teeth">baby teeth</a> of children across North America.  The "<a href="/wiki/Baby_Tooth_Survey" title="Baby Tooth Survey">Baby Tooth Survey</a>," published by Dr. <a href="/wiki/Louise_Reiss" title="Louise Reiss">Louise Reiss</a>, demonstrated conclusively in 1961 that above-ground nuclear testing posed significant public health risks in the form of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fallout" title="Nuclear fallout">radioactive fallout</a> spread primarily via milk from cows that had ingested contaminated grass.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>  The Committee for Nuclear Information is frequently credited for its significant contribution to supporting the test ban,<sup id="cite_ref-McCormick_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McCormick-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> as is the ground-breaking research conducted by Dr. Reiss and the "Baby Tooth Survey".<sup id="cite_ref-activism_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-activism-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Public pressure and the frightening results of the CNI research subsequently led to a moratorium on above-ground nuclear weapons testing, followed by the <a href="/wiki/Partial_Test_Ban_Treaty" class="mw-redirect" title="Partial Test Ban Treaty">Partial Test Ban Treaty</a>, signed in 1963 by <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nikita_Khrushchev" title="Nikita Khrushchev">Nikita Khrushchev</a>.  On the day that the treaty went into force, October 10, 1963, the Nobel Prize Committee awarded Pauling the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Peace_Prize" title="Nobel Peace Prize">Nobel Peace Prize</a> for 1962.  (No prize had previously been awarded for that year.)<sup id="cite_ref-PaloAlto_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PaloAlto-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>  They described him as "Linus Carl Pauling, who ever since 1946 has campaigned ceaselessly, not only against nuclear weapons tests, not only against the spread of these armaments, not only against their very use, but against all warfare as a means of solving international conflicts."<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>  Pauling himself acknowledged his wife Ava's deep involvement in peace work, and regretted that she was not awarded the Nobel Peace Prize with him.<sup id="cite_ref-LPIBio_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LPIBio-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_criticism">Political criticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linus_Pauling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Political criticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Linus_Pauling%27s_beret_at_the_Nobel_Museum_(51969).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Linus_Pauling%27s_beret_at_the_Nobel_Museum_%2851969%29.jpg/220px-Linus_Pauling%27s_beret_at_the_Nobel_Museum_%2851969%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Linus_Pauling%27s_beret_at_the_Nobel_Museum_%2851969%29.jpg/330px-Linus_Pauling%27s_beret_at_the_Nobel_Museum_%2851969%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Linus_Pauling%27s_beret_at_the_Nobel_Museum_%2851969%29.jpg/440px-Linus_Pauling%27s_beret_at_the_Nobel_Museum_%2851969%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4608" data-file-height="3456" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Linus_Pauling%27s_beret_at_the_Nobel_Museum_(51969).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pauling's beret on display at the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_Museum" title="Nobel Prize Museum">Nobel Prize Museum</a></div></div></div>
<p>Many of Pauling's critics, including scientists who appreciated the contributions that he had made in chemistry, disagreed with his political positions and saw him as a naïve spokesman for <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Communist Party of the Soviet Union">Soviet communism</a>. In 1960, he was ordered to appear before the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_Subcommittee_on_Internal_Security" title="United States Senate Subcommittee on Internal Security">Senate Internal Security Subcommittee</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Senate_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Senate-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> which termed him "the number one scientific name in virtually every major activity of the Communist peace offensive in this country."<sup id="cite_ref-Humanism_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Humanism-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> A headline in <i><a href="/wiki/Life_magazine" class="mw-redirect" title="Life magazine">Life</a></i> magazine characterized his 1962 Nobel Prize as "A Weird Insult from Norway".<sup id="cite_ref-Kovac_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kovac-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Life_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Life-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Pauling was a frequent target of the <i><a href="/wiki/National_Review" title="National Review">National Review</a></i> magazine. In an article entitled "The Collaborators" in the magazine's July 17, 1962 issue, Pauling was referred to not only as a collaborator, but as a "fellow traveler" of proponents of Soviet-style communism.  In 1965, Pauling sued the magazine, its publisher <a href="/wiki/William_Rusher" class="mw-redirect" title="William Rusher">William Rusher</a>, and its editor <a href="/wiki/William_F._Buckley,_Jr" class="mw-redirect" title="William F. Buckley, Jr">William F. Buckley, Jr</a> for $1 million. He lost both his libel suits and the 1968 appeal.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>His peace activism, his frequent travels, and his enthusiastic expansion into chemical-biomedical research all aroused opposition at Caltech. In 1958, the Caltech Board of Trustees demanded that Pauling step down as chairman of the Chemistry and Chemical Engineering Division.<sup id="cite_ref-LATimes1994_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LATimes1994-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>2</span></sup>  Although he had retained tenure as a full professor, Pauling chose to resign from Caltech after he received the Nobel peace prize money. He spent the next three years at the <a href="/wiki/Center_for_the_Study_of_Democratic_Institutions" title="Center for the Study of Democratic Institutions">Center for the Study of Democratic Institutions</a> (1963–1967).<sup id="cite_ref-Abrams_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abrams-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> In 1967, he moved to the University of California at San Diego, but remained there only briefly, leaving in 1969 in part because of political tensions with the Reagan-era board of regents.<sup id="cite_ref-LATimes1994_78-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LATimes1994-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>3</span></sup>  From 1969 to 1974, he accepted a position as Professor of Chemistry at Stanford University.<sup id="cite_ref-OH_43-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OH-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vietnam_war_activism">Vietnam war activism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linus_Pauling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Vietnam war activism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>During the 1960s, President Lyndon Johnson's policy of increasing America's involvement in the Vietnam War caused an <a href="/wiki/Anti-war_movement" title="Anti-war movement">anti-war movement</a> that the Paulings joined with enthusiasm. Pauling denounced the war as unnecessary and unconstitutional. He made speeches, signed protest letters and communicated personally with the North Vietnamese leader, Ho Chi Minh, and gave the lengthy written response to President Johnson. His efforts were ignored by the American government.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>
