</p><p>Pauling was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Lenin_Peace_Prize" title="Lenin Peace Prize">International Lenin Peace Prize</a> by the USSR in 1970.<sup id="cite_ref-Humanism_123-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Humanism-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-LeninPrize_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LeninPrize-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>  He continued his peace activism in the following years.  He and his wife Ava helped to found the <a href="/wiki/International_League_of_Humanists" title="International League of Humanists">International League of Humanists</a> in 1974.<sup id="cite_ref-HumanistFounders_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HumanistFounders-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> He was president of the scientific advisory board of the <a href="/wiki/World_Union_for_Protection_of_Life" title="World Union for Protection of Life">World Union for Protection of Life</a> and also one of the signatories of the Dubrovnik-Philadelphia Statement of 1974/1976.<sup id="cite_ref-Dubrovnik_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dubrovnik-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> Linus Carl Pauling was an honorary president and member of the International Academy of Science, Munich until the end of his life.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eugenics">Eugenics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linus_Pauling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Eugenics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Pauling supported a limited form of <a href="/wiki/Eugenics" title="Eugenics">eugenics</a> by suggesting that human carriers of defective genes be given a compulsory visible mark – such as a forehead tattoo – to discourage potential mates with the same defect, in order to reduce the number of babies with diseases such as <a href="/wiki/Sickle_cell_anemia" class="mw-redirect" title="Sickle cell anemia">sickle cell anemia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medical_research_and_vitamin_C_advocacy">Medical research and vitamin C advocacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linus_Pauling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Medical research and vitamin C advocacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pauling_Vit_C_Book_Cover.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/eb/Pauling_Vit_C_Book_Cover.jpg/150px-Pauling_Vit_C_Book_Cover.jpg" decoding="async" width="150" height="244" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/eb/Pauling_Vit_C_Book_Cover.jpg/225px-Pauling_Vit_C_Book_Cover.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/e/eb/Pauling_Vit_C_Book_Cover.jpg 2x" data-file-width="247" data-file-height="401" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pauling_Vit_C_Book_Cover.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pauling's book, <i>How to Live Longer and Feel Better</i>, advocated very high intake of <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_C" title="Vitamin C">Vitamin C</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In 1941, at age 40, Pauling was diagnosed with <a href="/wiki/Bright%27s_disease" title="Bright&#39;s disease">Bright's disease</a>, a renal disease. Following the recommendations of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Addis" title="Thomas Addis">Thomas Addis</a>, who actively recruited Ava Helen Pauling as "nutritionist, cook, and eventually as deputy 'doctor'", Pauling was able to control the disease with Addis's then-unusual low-protein salt-free diet and vitamin supplements.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> Thus Pauling's initial – and intensely personal – exposure to the idea of treating disease with vitamin supplements was positive.
</p><p>In 1965, Pauling read <i>Niacin Therapy in Psychiatry</i> by <a href="/wiki/Abram_Hoffer" title="Abram Hoffer">Abram Hoffer</a> and theorized vitamins might have important biochemical effects unrelated to their prevention of associated deficiency diseases.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> In 1968, Pauling published a brief paper in <a href="/wiki/Science_(journal)" title="Science (journal)"><i>Science</i></a> entitled "Orthomolecular psychiatry",<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> giving a name to the popular but controversial <a href="/wiki/Megavitamin_therapy" title="Megavitamin therapy">megavitamin therapy</a> movement of the 1970s, and advocating that "orthomolecular therapy, the provision for the individual person of the optimum concentrations of important normal constituents of the brain, may be the preferred treatment for many mentally ill patients." Pauling coined the term "orthomolecular" to refer to the practice of varying the concentration of substances normally present in the body to prevent and treat disease. His ideas formed the basis of <a href="/wiki/Orthomolecular_medicine" title="Orthomolecular medicine">orthomolecular medicine</a>, which is not generally practiced by conventional medical professionals and has been strongly criticized.<sup id="cite_ref-Cassileth_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cassileth-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bccancer_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bccancer-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1973, with <a href="/wiki/Arthur_B._Robinson" class="mw-redirect" title="Arthur B. Robinson">Arthur B. Robinson</a> and another colleague, Pauling founded the Institute of Orthomolecular Medicine in Menlo Park, California, which was soon renamed the <a href="/wiki/Linus_Pauling_Institute" title="Linus Pauling Institute">Linus Pauling Institute</a> of Science and Medicine. Pauling directed research on vitamin C, but also continued his theoretical work in chemistry and physics until his death. In his last years, he became especially interested in the possible role of vitamin C in preventing <a href="/wiki/Atherosclerosis" title="Atherosclerosis">atherosclerosis</a> and published three case reports on the use of <a href="/wiki/Lysine" title="Lysine">lysine</a> and vitamin C to relieve <a href="/wiki/Angina_pectoris" class="mw-redirect" title="Angina pectoris">angina pectoris</a>. During the 1990s, Pauling put forward a comprehensive plan for the treatment of heart disease using lysine and vitamin C. In 1996, a website was created expounding Pauling's treatment which it referred to as Pauling Therapy. Proponents of Pauling Therapy believe that  heart disease can be treated and even cured using only lysine and Vitamin C and without drugs or heart operations.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Pauling's work on <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_C" title="Vitamin C">vitamin C</a> in his later years generated much controversy. He was first introduced to the concept of high-dose vitamin C by biochemist <a href="/wiki/Irwin_Stone" title="Irwin Stone">Irwin Stone</a> in 1966. After becoming convinced of its worth, Pauling took 3&#160;grams of vitamin C every day to prevent <a href="/wiki/Colds" class="mw-redirect" title="Colds">colds</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-frs_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frs-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Excited by his own perceived results, he researched the clinical literature and published <i><a href="/wiki/Vitamin_C_and_the_Common_Cold_(book)" title="Vitamin C and the Common Cold (book)">Vitamin C and the Common Cold</a></i> in 1970. He began a long clinical collaboration with the British cancer surgeon <a href="/wiki/Ewan_Cameron_(physician)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ewan Cameron (physician)">Ewan Cameron</a> in 1971 on the use of intravenous and oral vitamin C as cancer therapy for terminal patients.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> Cameron and Pauling wrote many technical papers and a popular book, <i>Cancer and Vitamin C</i>, that discussed their observations. Pauling made vitamin C popular with the public<sup id="cite_ref-OnThisDay_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OnThisDay-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> and eventually published two studies of a group of 100 allegedly <a href="/wiki/Terminal_illness" title="Terminal illness">terminal</a> patients that claimed vitamin C increased survival by as much as four times compared to untreated patients.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A re-evaluation of the claims in 1982 found that the patient groups were not actually comparable, with the vitamin C group being less sick on entry to the study, and judged to be "terminal" much earlier than the comparison group.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> Later clinical trials conducted by the <a href="/wiki/Mayo_Clinic" title="Mayo Clinic">Mayo Clinic</a> also concluded that high-dose (10,000&#160;mg) vitamin C was no better than <a href="/wiki/Placebo" title="Placebo">placebo</a> at treating cancer and that there was no benefit to high-dose vitamin C.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> The failure of the clinical trials to demonstrate any benefit resulted in the conclusion that vitamin C was not effective in treating cancer; the medical establishment concluded that his claims that vitamin C could prevent colds or treat cancer were <a href="/wiki/Quackery" title="Quackery">quackery</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-frs_14-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frs-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-PNASChen2007_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PNASChen2007-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> Pauling denounced the conclusions of these studies and handling of the final study as "fraud and deliberate misrepresentation",<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Golem_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Golem-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> and criticized the studies for using oral, rather than <a href="/wiki/Intravenous_therapy" title="Intravenous therapy">intravenous</a> vitamin C<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> (which was the dosing method used for the first ten days of Pauling's original study<sup id="cite_ref-PNASChen2007_152-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PNASChen2007-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup>). Pauling also criticised the Mayo clinic studies because the controls were taking vitamin C during the trial, and because the duration of the treatment with vitamin C was short; Pauling advocated continued high-dose vitamin C for the rest of the cancer patient's life whereas the Mayo clinic patients in the second trial were treated with vitamin C for a median of 2.5 months.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> The results were publicly debated at length with considerable acrimony between Pauling and Cameron, and Moertel (the lead author of the Mayo Clinic studies), with accusations of misconduct and scientific incompetence on both sides.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This implies a balance that isn&#39;t evident but is pertinent. (July 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Ultimately the negative findings of the Mayo Clinic studies ended general interest in vitamin C as a treatment for cancer.<sup id="cite_ref-Golem_154-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Golem-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> Despite this, Pauling continued to promote vitamin C for treating cancer and the common cold, working with <a href="/wiki/The_Institutes_for_the_Achievement_of_Human_Potential" title="The Institutes for the Achievement of Human Potential">The Institutes for the Achievement of Human Potential</a> to use vitamin C in the treatment of brain-injured children.<sup id="cite_ref-Pauling1978_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pauling1978-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> He later collaborated with the Canadian physician <a href="/wiki/Abram_Hoffer" title="Abram Hoffer">Abram Hoffer</a> on a micronutrient regime, including high-dose vitamin C, as adjunctive cancer therapy.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> A 2009 review also noted differences between the studies, such as the Mayo clinic not using intravenous Vitamin C, and suggested further studies into the role of vitamin C when given intravenously.<sup id="cite_ref-Cancerreevaluated_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cancerreevaluated-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup>  Results from most clinical trials suggest that modest vitamin C supplementation alone or with other nutrients offers no benefit in the prevention of cancer.<sup id="cite_ref-Oncologist_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oncologist-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NIHFactsheet_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NIHFactsheet-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linus_Pauling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Personal life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Linus_Pauling_family_1954.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Linus_Pauling_family_1954.jpg/300px-Linus_Pauling_family_1954.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="267" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Linus_Pauling_family_1954.jpg/450px-Linus_Pauling_family_1954.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Linus_Pauling_family_1954.jpg/600px-Linus_Pauling_family_1954.jpg 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="801" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Linus_Pauling_family_1954.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Pauling children at a gathering in celebration of the 1954 Nobel Prizes in Stockholm, Sweden. Seated from left: Linus Pauling, Jr., Peter Pauling and Linda Pauling. Standing from left: an unidentified person, and Crellin Pauling</div></div></div>
<p>Pauling married <a href="/wiki/Ava_Helen_Pauling" title="Ava Helen Pauling">Ava Helen Miller</a> on June 17, 1923. The marriage lasted until Ava Pauling's death in 1981. They had four children.<sup id="cite_ref-family_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-family-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> Linus Carl Jr. (born 1925) became a <a href="/wiki/Psychiatry" title="Psychiatry">psychiatrist</a>; Peter (1931–2003) a <a href="/wiki/Crystallography" title="Crystallography">crystallographer</a> at <a href="/wiki/University_College_London" title="University College London">University College London</a>; Edward Crellin Pauling (1937–1997) a <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biologist</a>; and Linda Helen (born 1932) married noted Caltech geologist and glaciologist <a href="/wiki/Barclay_Kamb" title="Barclay Kamb">Barclay Kamb</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Pauling was raised as a member of the <a href="/wiki/Lutheran" class="mw-redirect" title="Lutheran">Lutheran</a> Church,<sup id="cite_ref-AIPOralHistory_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AIPOralHistory-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> but later joined the <a href="/wiki/Unitarian_Universalist" class="mw-redirect" title="Unitarian Universalist">Unitarian Universalist</a> Church.<sup id="cite_ref-UUBio_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UUBio-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> Two years before his death, in a published dialogue with Buddhist philosopher <a href="/wiki/Daisaku_Ikeda" title="Daisaku Ikeda">Daisaku Ikeda</a>, Pauling publicly declared his <a href="/wiki/Atheism" title="Atheism">atheism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On January 30, 1960, Pauling and his wife were using a cabin about 80 miles (130&#160;km) south of <a href="/wiki/Monterey,_California" title="Monterey, California">Monterey</a>, California, and he decided to go for a walk on a coastal trail.  He got lost and tried to climb the rocky cliff, but reached a large overhanging rock about 300 feet (90&#160;m) above the ocean.  He decided it was safest to stay there, and meanwhile he was reported missing.  He spent a sleepless night on the cliff before being found after almost 24 hours.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Death_and_legacy">Death and legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linus_Pauling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Death and legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Pauling died of <a href="/wiki/Prostate_cancer" title="Prostate cancer">prostate cancer</a> on August 19, 1994, at 19:20 at home in <a href="/wiki/Big_Sur" title="Big Sur">Big Sur</a>, California.<sup id="cite_ref-Offit_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Offit-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> He was 93 years old.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> A grave marker for Pauling was placed in Oswego Pioneer Cemetery in <a href="/wiki/Lake_Oswego,_Oregon" title="Lake Oswego, Oregon">Lake Oswego</a>, Oregon by his sister Pauline, but Pauling's ashes, along with those of his wife, were not buried there until 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-grave_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grave-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Pauling's discoveries led to decisive contributions in a  diverse array of areas including around 350 publications in the fields of quantum mechanics, inorganic chemistry, organic chemistry, protein structure, molecular biology, and medicine.<sup id="cite_ref-California_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-California-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>His work on chemical bonding marks him as one of the founders of modern <a href="/wiki/Quantum_chemistry" title="Quantum chemistry">quantum chemistry</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-natureobit_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-natureobit-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> <i>The Nature of the Chemical Bond</i> was the standard work for many years,<sup id="cite_ref-Hamilton_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hamilton-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> and concepts like <a href="/wiki/Orbital_hybridisation" title="Orbital hybridisation">hybridization</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electronegativity" title="Electronegativity">electronegativity</a> remain part of standard chemistry textbooks. While his <a href="/wiki/Valence_bond" class="mw-redirect" title="Valence bond">Valence bond</a> approach fell short of accounting quantitatively for some of the characteristics of molecules, such as the color of <a href="/wiki/Organometallic" class="mw-redirect" title="Organometallic">organometallic</a> complexes, and would later be eclipsed by the <a href="/wiki/Molecular_orbital_theory" title="Molecular orbital theory">molecular orbital theory</a> of <a href="/wiki/Robert_Mulliken" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert Mulliken">Robert Mulliken</a>, Valence Bond Theory still competes, in its modern form, with Molecular Orbital Theory and <a href="/wiki/Density_functional_theory" title="Density functional theory">density functional theory</a> (DFT) as a way of describing the chemical phenomena.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> Pauling's work on crystal structure contributed significantly to the prediction and elucidation of the structures of complex minerals and compounds.<sup id="cite_ref-Marinacci_35-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marinacci-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>80–81</span></sup> His discovery of the alpha helix and beta sheet is a fundamental foundation for the study of protein structure.<sup id="cite_ref-Goertzel_and_Goertzel,_p._95-100_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goertzel_and_Goertzel,_p._95-100-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Francis_Crick" title="Francis Crick">Francis Crick</a> acknowledged Pauling as the "father of <a href="/wiki/Molecular_biology" title="Molecular biology">molecular biology</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-natureobit_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-natureobit-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> His discovery of <a href="/wiki/Sickle_cell_anemia" class="mw-redirect" title="Sickle cell anemia">sickle cell anemia</a> as a "molecular disease" opened the way toward examining genetically acquired mutations at a molecular level.<sup id="cite_ref-Strasser_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Strasser-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Pauling's 1951 publication with Robert B. Corey and H. R. Branson, "The Structure of Proteins: Two Hydrogen-Bonded Helical Configurations of the Polypeptide Chain," was a key early finding in the then newly emerging field of molecular biology. This publication was honored by a Citation for Chemical Breakthrough Award from the Division of History of Chemistry of the American Chemical Society presented to the Department of Chemistry, Caltech, in 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-breakthrough_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-breakthrough-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-configurations_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-configurations-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Commemorations">Commemorations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linus_Pauling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Commemorations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Oregon State University completed construction of the $77 million, 100,000-square-foot (9,300&#160;m<sup>2</sup>) Linus Pauling Science Center in the late 2000s, now housing a bulk of Oregon State's chemistry classrooms, labs, and instruments.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On March 6, 2008, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Postal_Service" title="United States Postal Service">United States Postal Service</a> released a 41 cent stamp honoring Pauling designed by artist <a href="/wiki/Victor_Stabin" title="Victor Stabin">Victor Stabin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-stamp_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stamp-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> His description reads: "A remarkably versatile scientist, structural chemist Linus Pauling (1901–1994) won the 1954 Nobel Prize in Chemistry for determining the nature of the chemical bond linking atoms into molecules. His work in establishing the field of molecular biology; his studies of hemoglobin led to the classification of sickle cell anemia as a molecular disease."<sup id="cite_ref-Strasser_77-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Strasser-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> The other scientists on this sheet of stamps included <a href="/wiki/Gerty_Cori" title="Gerty Cori">Gerty Cori</a>, biochemist, <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Hubble" title="Edwin Hubble">Edwin Hubble</a>, astronomer, and <a href="/wiki/John_Bardeen" title="John Bardeen">John Bardeen</a>, physicist.<sup id="cite_ref-stamp_179-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stamp-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>California Governor <a href="/wiki/Arnold_Schwarzenegger" title="Arnold Schwarzenegger">Arnold Schwarzenegger</a> and First Lady <a href="/wiki/Maria_Shriver" title="Maria Shriver">Maria Shriver</a> announced on May 28, 2008 that Pauling would be inducted into the <a href="/wiki/California_Hall_of_Fame" title="California Hall of Fame">California Hall of Fame</a>, located at <a href="/wiki/The_California_Museum_for_History,_Women_and_the_Arts" class="mw-redirect" title="The California Museum for History, Women and the Arts">The California Museum for History, Women and the Arts</a>. The induction ceremony took place December 15, 2008. Pauling's son was asked to accept the honor in his place.<sup id="cite_ref-CA.gov_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CA.gov-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By proclamation of Gov. <a href="/wiki/John_Kitzhaber" title="John Kitzhaber">John Kitzhaber</a> in the state of Oregon, February 28 has been named "Linus Pauling Day".<sup id="cite_ref-Notebooks_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Notebooks-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup>  The Linus Pauling Institute still exists, but moved in 1996 from Palo Alto, California, to Corvallis, Oregon, where it is part of the Linus Pauling Science Center at <a href="/wiki/Oregon_State_University" title="Oregon State University">Oregon State University</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CGT2011_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CGT2011-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-OSUFoundation_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OSUFoundation-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_Valley_Library" title="The Valley Library">The Valley Library</a> Special Collections at Oregon State University contain the Ava Helen and Linus Pauling Papers, including digitized versions of Pauling's forty-six research notebooks.<sup id="cite_ref-Notebooks_181-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Notebooks-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1986, Caltech commemorated Linus Pauling with a Symposium and Lectureship.<sup id="cite_ref-Zewail_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zewail-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> The Pauling Lecture series at Caltech began in 1989 with a lecture by Pauling himself.  The Caltech Chemistry Department renamed room 22 of Gates Hall the Linus Pauling Lecture Hall, since Linus spent so much time there.<sup id="cite_ref-Baum_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baum-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Other places named after Pauling include Pauling Street in Foothill Ranch, California;<sup id="cite_ref-Factory_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Factory-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> Linus Pauling Drive in Hercules, California; Linus and Ava Helen Pauling Hall at <a href="/wiki/Soka_University_of_America" title="Soka University of America">Soka University of America</a> in Aliso Viejo, California;<sup id="cite_ref-Gottlieb_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gottlieb-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> Linus Pauling Middle School in Corvallis, Oregon;<sup id="cite_ref-Woodward_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Woodward-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Condon_State_Airport" title="Condon State Airport">Pauling Field</a>, a small airfield located in Condon, Oregon, where Pauling spent his youth.<sup id="cite_ref-Airport_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Airport-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup>  There is a psychedelic rock band in Houston, Texas, named <a href="/wiki/The_Linus_Pauling_Quartet" title="The Linus Pauling Quartet">The Linus Pauling Quartet</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Heberlein_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Heberlein-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The asteroid <a href="/wiki/4674_Pauling" title="4674 Pauling">4674 Pauling</a> in the inner asteroid belt, discovered by <a href="/wiki/Eleanor_F._Helin" title="Eleanor F. Helin">Eleanor F. Helin</a>, was named after Linus Pauling in 1991, on his 90th birthday.<sup id="cite_ref-Schmadel_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schmadel-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Linus_Torvalds" title="Linus Torvalds">Linus Torvalds</a>, developer of the <a href="/wiki/Linux" title="Linux">Linux</a> kernel, is named after Pauling.<sup id="cite_ref-moody_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moody-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Nobel laureate <a href="/wiki/Peter_Agre" title="Peter Agre">Peter Agre</a> has said that Linus Pauling inspired him.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2010, <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Northwest_National_Laboratory" title="Pacific Northwest National Laboratory">Pacific Northwest National Laboratory</a> named its distinguished postdoctoral program in his honor, as the <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.pnnl.gov/pauling">Linus Pauling Distinguished Postdoctoral Fellowship Program</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Honors_and_awards">Honors and awards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linus_Pauling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Honors and awards">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Pauling received numerous awards and honors during his career, including the following:<sup id="cite_ref-summary_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-summary-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-OH_43-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OH-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-honors_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-honors-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em;">
<ul><li>1931 <a href="/wiki/ACS_Award_in_Pure_Chemistry" title="ACS Award in Pure Chemistry">ACS Award in Pure Chemistry</a> <sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1931 <a href="/wiki/Irving_Langmuir_Award" title="Irving Langmuir Award">Irving Langmuir Award</a>, American Chemical Society.<sup id="cite_ref-OH_43-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OH-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-honors_196-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-honors-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1940 <a href="/wiki/Alpha_Chi_Sigma" title="Alpha Chi Sigma">Alpha Chi Sigma</a>, professional chemistry fraternity.<sup id="cite_ref-Chi1940_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chi1940-198">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1941 <a href="/wiki/William_H._Nichols#Nichols&#39;_legacy" title="William H. Nichols">Nichols Medal</a>, New York Section, American Chemical Society.<sup id="cite_ref-OH_43-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OH-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1946 <a href="/wiki/Willard_Gibbs_Award" title="Willard Gibbs Award">Willard Gibbs Award</a>, Chicago section of the American Chemical Society.<sup id="cite_ref-honors_196-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-honors-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1947 <a href="/wiki/Davy_Medal" title="Davy Medal">Davy Medal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-OH_43-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OH-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-honors_196-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-honors-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1947 T. W. Richards Medal, Northeastern Section of the American Chemical Society.<sup id="cite_ref-honors_196-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-honors-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1948 <a href="/wiki/Presidential_Medal_for_Merit" class="mw-redirect" title="Presidential Medal for Merit">Presidential Medal for Merit</a> by President <a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman" title="Harry S. Truman">Harry S. Truman</a> of the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-OH_43-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OH-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-honors_196-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-honors-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1948 Elected a Foreign Member of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a> of London (ForMemRS)<sup id="cite_ref-frs_14-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frs-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1951 <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_N._Lewis" title="Gilbert N. Lewis">Gilbert N. Lewis</a> medal, California section of the American Chemical Society.<sup id="cite_ref-honors_196-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-honors-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1952 Pasteur Medal, Biochemical Society of France.<sup id="cite_ref-OH_43-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OH-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1954 <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Chemistry" title="Nobel Prize in Chemistry">Nobel Prize in Chemistry</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-OH_43-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OH-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-honors_196-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-honors-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1955 Addis Medal, National Nephrosis Foundation.<sup id="cite_ref-OH_43-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OH-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-honors_196-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-honors-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1955 John Phillips Memorial Award, <a href="/wiki/American_College_of_Physicians" title="American College of Physicians">American College of Physicians</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-OH_43-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OH-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-honors_196-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-honors-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1956 Avogadro Medal, Italian Academy of Science.<sup id="cite_ref-OH_43-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OH-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-honors_196-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-honors-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1957 <a href="/wiki/Paul_Sabatier_(chemist)" title="Paul Sabatier (chemist)">Paul Sabatier</a> Medal.</li>
<li>1957 Pierre Fermat Medal in Mathematics (awarded for only the sixth time in three centuries).<sup id="cite_ref-OH_43-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OH-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-honors_196-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-honors-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1957 International <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Grotius" title="Hugo Grotius">Grotius</a> Medal.<sup id="cite_ref-OH_43-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OH-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1959 <a href="/wiki/Messenger_Lectures" title="Messenger Lectures">Messenger Lectureship</a></li>
<li>1960 Fellow, <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society_of_Arts" title="Royal Society of Arts">Royal Society of Arts</a></li>
<li>1961 Humanist of the Year, <a href="/wiki/American_Humanist_Association" title="American Humanist Association">American Humanist Association</a>.</li>
<li>1961 <a href="/wiki/Gandhi_Peace_Award" title="Gandhi Peace Award">Gandhi Peace Award</a> by <a href="/wiki/Promoting_Enduring_Peace" title="Promoting Enduring Peace">Promoting Enduring Peace</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1962 <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Peace_Prize" title="Nobel Peace Prize">Nobel Peace Prize</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-OH_43-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OH-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-honors_196-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-honors-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1965 Medal, Academy of the Rumanian People's Republic.<sup id="cite_ref-OH_43-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OH-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1966 <a href="/wiki/Linus_Pauling_Award" title="Linus Pauling Award">Linus Pauling Award</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-OH_43-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OH-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1966 Silver Medal, <a href="/wiki/Institute_of_France" class="mw-redirect" title="Institute of France">Institute of France</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-OH_43-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OH-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1966 Supreme Peace Sponsor, World Fellowship of Religion.<sup id="cite_ref-OH_43-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OH-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1967 <a href="/wiki/Washington_A._Roebling" class="mw-redirect" title="Washington A. Roebling">Washington A. Roebling</a> Medal, <a href="/wiki/Mineralogical_Society_of_America" title="Mineralogical Society of America">Mineralogical Society of America</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-honors_196-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-honors-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1972 <a href="/wiki/Lenin_Peace_Prize" title="Lenin Peace Prize">Lenin Peace Prize</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-OH_43-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OH-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1974 <a href="/wiki/National_Medal_of_Science" title="National Medal of Science">National Medal of Science</a> by President <a href="/wiki/Gerald_R._Ford" class="mw-redirect" title="Gerald R. Ford">Gerald R. Ford</a> of the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-honors_196-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-honors-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1978 <a href="/wiki/Lomonosov_Gold_Medal" title="Lomonosov Gold Medal">Lomonosov Gold Medal</a>, Presidium of the Academy of the USSR.<sup id="cite_ref-OH_43-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OH-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-honors_196-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-honors-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1979 Gold Medal Honoree, <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_of_Social_Sciences" title="National Institute of Social Sciences">National Institute of Social Sciences</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1979 <a href="/wiki/NAS_Award_in_Chemical_Sciences" title="NAS Award in Chemical Sciences">NAS Award in Chemical Sciences</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States_National_Academy_of_Sciences" class="mw-redirect" title="United States National Academy of Sciences">National Academy of Sciences</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-OH_43-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OH-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Chemical_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chemical-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1979 Golden Plate Award, <a href="/wiki/American_Academy_of_Achievement" class="mw-redirect" title="American Academy of Achievement">American Academy of Achievement</a> <sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1981 <a href="/wiki/John_K._Lattimer" title="John K. Lattimer">John K. Lattimer</a> Award, <a href="/wiki/American_Urological_Association" title="American Urological Association">American Urological Association</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-honors_196-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-honors-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1984 <a href="/wiki/Priestley_Medal" title="Priestley Medal">Priestley Medal</a>, American Chemical Society.<sup id="cite_ref-OH_43-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OH-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-honors_196-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-honors-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1984 Award for Chemistry, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_M._Sackler" title="Arthur M. Sackler">Arthur M. Sackler</a> Foundation.<sup id="cite_ref-OH_43-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OH-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1986 <a href="/wiki/Lavoisier_Medal" title="Lavoisier Medal">Lavoisier Medal</a> by Fondation de la Maison de la Chimie.<sup id="cite_ref-honors_196-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-honors-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1987 Award in Chemical Education, American Chemical Society.<sup id="cite_ref-OH_43-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OH-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1989 <a href="/wiki/Vannevar_Bush_Award" title="Vannevar Bush Award">Vannevar Bush Award</a>, <a href="/wiki/National_Science_Board" title="National Science Board">National Science Board</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-OH_43-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OH-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-honors_196-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-honors-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1990 <a href="/wiki/Richard_C._Tolman" title="Richard C. Tolman">Richard C. Tolman</a> Medal, American Chemical Society Southern California Section.<sup id="cite_ref-OH_43-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OH-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1992 <a href="/wiki/Daisaku_Ikeda" title="Daisaku Ikeda">Daisaku Ikeda</a> Medal, <a href="/wiki/Soka_Gakkai_International" title="Soka Gakkai International">Soka Gakkai International</a><sup id="cite_ref-honors_196-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-honors-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>2008 "American Scientists" <a href="/wiki/List_of_people_on_stamps_of_the_United_States#P" class="mw-redirect" title="List of people on stamps of the United States">U.S. postage stamp</a> series, $0.41, for his sickle cell disease work.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
</div>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Books">Books</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linus_Pauling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Books">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<li id="cite_note-104"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-104">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web cs1"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://usstampgallery.com/view.php?id=3228fbec24ea876e8035f61b5fcd643af29a611c">"Linus Pauling"</a>. <i>U.S. Stamp Gallery</i><span class="reference-accessdate">. I have never been guilty of any unpatriotic or criminal act."</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=U.S.+Stamp+Gallery&amp;rft.atitle=Linus+Pauling&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fusstampgallery.com%2Fview.php%3Fid%3D3228fbec24ea876e8035f61b5fcd643af29a611c&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALinus+Pauling" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r982806391"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-NIHFactsheet-161"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-NIHFactsheet_161-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web cs1"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://ods.od.nih.gov/factsheets/VitaminC-HealthProfessional/">"Vitamin C Fact Sheet for Health Professionals"</a>. <i>National Institutes of Health</i><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">June 2,</span> 2015</span>. <q>"At this time, the evidence is inconsistent on whether dietary vitamin C intake affects cancer risk. Results from most clinical trials suggest that modest vitamin C supplementation alone or with other nutrients offers no benefit in the prevention of cancer... Some researchers support reassessment of the use of high-dose IV vitamin C as a drug to treat cancer...  It is uncertain whether supplemental vitamin C and other antioxidants might interact with chemotherapy and/or radiation.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=National+Institutes+of+Health&amp;rft.atitle=Vitamin+C+Fact+Sheet+for+Health+Professionals&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fods.od.nih.gov%2Ffactsheets%2FVitaminC-HealthProfessional%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALinus+Pauling" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r982806391"/></span>
